Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the difference between nubuwwa and risāla is a core doctrinal issue that must be definitively settled.
Correction: Buti clarifies that this is a scholarly ijtihadī matter, not a definitive matter of religion, so disagreement is permissible.

Checkpoint
According to the first opinion mentioned, what is the relationship between nubuwwa and risāla?
A) They are synonymous, applied to the same person but from different perspectives.
B) They are mutually exclusive.
C) Nubuwwa is a subset of risāla.
D) Risāla is a subset of nubuwwa.

Key Takeaway
Prophethood and messengerhood are distinct concepts: prophethood is the reception of revelation from God, while messengerhood is the commission to convey it to people, and their relationship is subject to scholarly debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to follow the Prophet (peace be upon him). But which relationship with him do you prioritize: the one that links you to God through his prophethood, or the one that links you to his message to people? Have you ever stopped to think which part of his mission you are actually trying to embody today? Resolve this very moment to connect to him through the higher link—the one with Allah—before you engage with the secondary link.
?
The author says the difference between nubuwwa and risāla is not a definitive matter, yet how much of your energy have you wasted in arguments over secondary issues while neglecting the essential message of tawḥīd and submission? When will you repent from prioritizing scholarly debates over personal transformation? Make a firm intention now to focus on what brings you closer to Allah.
?
If prophethood is the link with God, is your own connection with Him through revelation truly alive? Or have you reduced religion to mere transactions between people—empty rituals and social duties—while your heart remains disconnected from the One who sent the message? Ask yourself: what single change can you make today to turn your attention back to the vertical relationship with your Lord?

Definition of Prophethood and the Nature of Wahy
Buti presents the agreed definition of nubuwwa and risāla and clarifies the nature of wahy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the agreed definition of a prophet and messenger in Islam, and the two key clarifications about the nature of wahy: its distinction from ordinary inspiration and its exclusive occurrence in full wakefulness.

Core Idea
Prophetic revelation comes through Jibril in full wakefulness, not through dreams or inner feelings.

Buti presents the consensus definition of prophet and messenger that all Muslims accept. This definition is foundational for understanding prophethood. After reading the definition, he draws two important conclusions that protect the concept of wahy from common misconceptions. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Focus on the definition of prophet and messenger, noting the role of Jibril and the condition of conveying a message.
The definition distinguishes between a prophet (who receives revelation but is not commanded to convey) and a messenger (who must convey).
2
Understand the first clarification
Buti states that the term 'wahy' in this religious context does not include common senses like inspiration, inner feeling, clairvoyance, or intuition. Reflect on why this distinction is important to avoid confusion.
Even though the word 'wahy' is used broadly in ordinary language, its technical meaning is restricted.
3
Understand the second clarification
Buti emphasizes that prophetic revelation only occurs in a state of full wakefulness, not in dreams or visions. Consider how this sets prophethood apart from other types of spiritual experience.
This reinforces that the prophet is fully conscious when receiving revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا فإننا نعرف كلاً من النبي والرسول بأنه: (إنسان أوحى الله إليه بواسطة جبريل أن يبلغ عامة الناس أو فئة منهم أمراً من قبل الله جل جلاله، فإن أوحى الله إليه بأمر ولم يأمره بتبليغه فهو نبي فقط). وإذا تأملت في هذا التعريف الذي أجمع على مضمونه المسلمون كلهم، ودلت عليه قواطع الأدلة كما سنعرض لك، علمت ما يلي: أولاً - ليس الوحي المعني هنا ما يشمل الإلهام والشعور الباطني وما يسمى بالفراسة والحدس، وإن كانت كلمة الوحي تطلق على كل ذلك في مخاطبات الناس واصطلاحاتهم. وسيأتي تحقيق ذلك قريباً. ثانياً - لا مجال لهذا الوحي في ابتدائه إلا حال اليقظة التامة، فليس للرؤى والأحلام إذ ذاك أي علاقة بإثبات معنى النبوة أو الوحي الإلهي الذي يعتبر. (انظر حاشية المرجاني على شرح الجلال الدواني على العقائد العضدية: 1-12 وغيرها من كتب العقيدة)
And on this basis, we define each of the prophet and the messenger as: 'A human being to whom God revealed by means of Jibrīl to convey to the general people or a group of them a matter from God, Exalted is His Majesty. If God revealed to him a matter and did not command him to convey it, then he is only a prophet.' And if you reflect on this definition, upon whose content all Muslims have agreed, and upon which the definitive proofs indicate, as we will present to you, you will know the following: First – the revelation (al-waḥy) intended here does not include inspiration (al-ilhām), inner feeling, what is called clairvoyance (al-firāsa) or intuition (al-ḥads), even though the word revelation is applied to all that in people's speech and conventions. Its verification will come shortly. Second – this revelation, in its initiation, has no place except in a state of full wakefulness; so visions and dreams have no relationship at all to establishing the meaning of prophethood or divine revelation which is considered. (See al-Marjānī's gloss on al-Dawānī's commentary on al-ʿAḍudiyya: 1-12 and other books of creed.)
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents the agreed definition of prophet and messenger accepted by all Muslims, and then clarifies two important points about the nature of wahy (revelation) related to prophethood. The author defines both prophet and messenger as a human being to whom God reveals by means of Jibrīl to convey a divine matter to people; if the person is not commanded to convey, he is a prophet only. This definition is comprehensive and agreed upon. Then he draws two conclusions: First, the term 'wahy' in this religious context is specific and does not include general senses like inspiration (ilhām), inner feeling, clairvoyance (firāsa), or intuition (ḥads), even though the word may be used broadly in ordinary language. Second, true divine revelation for prophethood only occurs in a state of full wakefulness, not through dreams or visions. The author cites a reference for further study on this point.

The Relationship Between Prophet and Messenger
Buti explains the majority view on the logical relationship between nabi and rasul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority scholarly view on the logical relationship between the terms 'prophet' (nabī) and 'messenger' (rasūl) and why this distinction is not a core creedal issue.

Core Idea
Every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger.

In this passage, Buti explains a key distinction in Islamic theology: the difference between a prophet (nabī) and a messenger (rasūl). While both receive divine revelation, the crucial factor is whether they are commanded to deliver it. This seemingly subtle point has been debated by scholars, but Buti emphasizes that it is not a matter of fundamental creed. Understanding this helps clarify the scope of prophethood and messengership in Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noticing key terms without analysis.
Focus on the definitions of nabī and rasūl.
2
Identify the majority view
Find the logical relationship expressed: every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger.
Note the term 'ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq' (absolute commonality and specificity).
3
Understand the definitions
A prophet receives inspiration; a messenger is a prophet commanded to deliver.
The key is the command to deliver.
4
Appreciate Buti's conclusion
Buti says this is a subsidiary issue, not a decisive creedal matter.
He refrains from weighing evidence because of its non-essential nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب كثيرون إلى أن بينهما عموماً وخصوصاً مطلقاً ، إذ النبي هو من أوحي إليه بأمر من الله ، سواء كلف بتبليغه أم لا ، فإن كلف بتبليغه كأن أوحي إليه بشرع أو كتاب إلى عامة الناس ، فهو رسول أيضاً . وعلى هذا ، فكل رسول نبي ، إذ الرسالة إلى الناس فرع عن النبوة من الله ، وليس كل نبي رسولا ، إذ قد يكون موحى إليه ، دون تكليف له بالتبليغ (۱) . ولكل من الفريقين أدلة من ظاهر الكتاب والسنة ، ولسنا بحاجة في هذا الصدد إلى عرض شيء منها ، أو إلى إطالة البحث بالترجيح . إذ الخطب يسير ما دام البحث كما قلنا لا يتعلق بشيء من قواطع الدين وضرورياته ، بل من الفروع الاجتهادية المحتملة
And many went to the view that between them there is absolute commonality and specificity. Indeed, the prophet (nabī) is the one who is inspired by God with a command, whether he is charged with delivering it or not. If he is charged with delivering it, such as being inspired with a law or a book to the general public, then he is also a messenger (rasūl). On this basis, every messenger is a prophet, because the message to people is a branch of prophethood from God, and not every prophet is a messenger, for he may be inspired without being charged with delivery (1). And each of the two groups has evidence from the apparent meaning of the Book and the Sunnah, and we have no need in this regard to present any of them, or to prolong research by weighing. Indeed, the matter is easy, as long as the research, as we have said, does not concern any of the decisive matters of religion (qawāṭiʿ al-dīn) and its necessities, but rather from the subsidiary ijtihādī possibilities.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents the scholarly opinion on the logical relationship between the terms 'prophet' (nabī) and 'messenger' (rasūl). Buti reports that many scholars hold that there is an absolute commonality and specificity (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq) between them: a prophet is any person who receives divine inspiration (waḥy) from God, whether or not he is commanded to deliver it. If he is commanded to deliver it to people, he is both a prophet and a messenger. Thus every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger. Buti notes that both views have evidence from the Qur'an and Sunnah, but he declines to elaborate because the issue is not one of the decisive, necessary matters of religion (qawāṭiʿ al-dīn wa ḍarūriyyātihī) but rather a subsidiary ijtihādī matter. The author's point is to de-emphasize the importance of this distinction for creedal purposes.

Key Terms
نبي (nabī)
رسول (rasūl)
وحي (waḥy)
عموماً وخصوصاً مطلقاً (ʿumūman wa khuṣūṣan muṭlaqan)
قواطع الدين (qawāṭiʿ al-dīn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The difference between a prophet and a messenger is a fundamental creedal issue that must be precisely determined.
Correction: Buti clarifies that this distinction is a subsidiary ijtihādī matter, not among the decisive matters of religion (qawāṭiʿ al-dīn).

Checkpoint
According to the majority view presented by Buti, what is the relationship between a prophet (nabī) and a messenger (rasūl)?
A) They are synonymous terms with no difference.
B) Every prophet is a messenger, but not every messenger is a prophet.
C) Every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger.
D) They are completely distinct and have no overlap.

Key Takeaway
The majority view holds that every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger, and this distinction is not a core creedal requirement but a subsidiary scholarly discussion.

The Unified Definition of Prophet and Messenger and Conditions of Wahy
Buti gives a unified definition and two clarifications regarding wahy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the unified definition of prophet and messenger and the two key clarifications regarding the nature of prophetic revelation (wahy).

Core Idea
Prophetic wahy is exclusively wakeful and does not include dreams or inner inspirations.

This lesson presents the definition of nabī and rasūl as given by Buti, which distinguishes them based on whether they are commanded to convey the revelation. It then clarifies two important aspects of wahy: it is not the same as inspiration or intuition, and it only occurs in a state of full wakefulness. Understanding these points is essential for grasping the foundations of prophethood in Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Buti defines both prophet and messenger in one definition, with the key condition of being commanded to convey.
The definition is: a human being who receives revelation via Jibrīl to convey to people; if not commanded to convey, he is only a prophet.
2
Understand the first clarification
The term 'wahy' here does not include inspiration (ilhām), inner feeling, clairvoyance, or intuition, even though in common usage it may cover these. Buti emphasizes that this is a specific technical meaning.
This prevents confusion with other types of divine communication.
3
Understand the second clarification
Prophetic wahy only occurs in a state of full wakefulness. Dreams and visions are not a source for proving prophethood. Once prophethood is established, dreams can be considered revelation if they do not contradict wakeful revelation.
This distinction safeguards the objective evidence for prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا فإننا نعرف كلاً من النبي والرسول بأنه : ( إنسان أوحى الله إليه بواسطة جبريل أن يبلغ عامة الناس أو فئة منهم أمراً من قبل الله جل جلاله فإن أوحى الله إليه بأمر ولم يأمره بتبليغه فهو نبي فقط ) وإذا تأملت في هذا التعريف الذي أجمع على مضمونه المسلمون كلهم ، ودلت عليه قواطع الأدلة كما سنعرض لك ، علمت ما يلي : أولاً - ليس الوحي المعني هنا ما يشمل الإلهام والشعور الباطني وما يسمى بالفراسة والحدس ، وإن كانت كلمة الوحي تطلق على كل ذلك في مخاطبات الناس واصطلاحاتهم . وسيأتي تحقيق ذلك قريباً ثانياً - لا مجال لهذا الوحي في ابتدائه إلا حال اليقظة التامة ، فليس للرؤى والأحلام إذ ذاك أي علاقة بإثبات معنى النبوة أو الوحي الإلهي الذي يعتبر الدعامة الأولى للنبوة ، فإذا ثبتت دلائل النبوة لنا ، فإن رؤى الأنبياء تعتبر بعد ذلك من الوحي ما لم يأت وحي في اليقظة يعارضه أو يرده . وليس البحث في هذه المسألة مما يتسع لأي احتمال أو نقاش فيه ( بالنسبة لمن سبق أن آمن بوجود الله جل جلاله ) . ولذلك فقد كان مضمون كلمة ( الرسالة الإلهية ( محل اتفاق من جميع فئات المسلمين وجماعاتهم ، وكان الاعتقاد بذلك من أسس التي لا بدَّ من الإيمان بها ليتم الإيمان بالله عز وجل .
And on this basis, we define each of the prophet and the messenger as: 'A human being to whom God revealed through Jibrīl to convey to the general public or a group of them a command from God—glorified and exalted be He. If God revealed to him a command and did not command him to convey it, he is only a prophet.' And if you reflect upon this definition, whose content all Muslims have agreed upon, and which the decisive evidences indicate as we will present to you, you will know the following: First: The revelation (waḥy) intended here does not include inspiration (ilhām), inner feeling (shuʿūr bāṭinī), what is called clairvoyance (farāsa), or intuition (ḥads), even though the word 'waḥy' is applied to all of that in people's speech and conventions. Its verification will come soon. Second: There is no scope for this revelation in its inception except in a state of full wakefulness (yaqaẓa tāmma). Dreams and visions (ruʾā wa aḥlām) have no relationship at that point to establishing the meaning of prophethood or the divine revelation that is considered the first pillar of prophethood. Once the evidences of prophethood are established for us, then the dreams of the prophets are after that considered revelation, as long as no wakeful revelation comes that contradicts or rejects it. And the research on this matter does not allow for any possibility or discussion (for one who has already believed in the existence of God—exalted be He). Therefore, the content of the phrase 'the divine message' (al-risāla al-ilāhiyya) was a point of agreement among all sects and groups of Muslims, and belief in it was among the foundations that must be believed in so that faith in God—mighty and majestic—is complete.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of prophet and messenger and then clarifies two important aspects of waḥy. Buti gives a combined definition: a human being who receives revelation from God via Jibrīl with a command to deliver to people. If no delivery command is given, he is only a prophet. Then he explains two points. First, the term 'waḥy' here does not include inspiration (ilhām), inner feeling, clairvoyance, or intuition, even though in common usage it may cover these. Second, prophetic waḥy only occurs in full wakefulness; dreams are not a source for proving prophethood. However, once prophethood is established, the dreams of prophets can be considered a form of waḥy as long as they do not contradict wakeful revelation. Buti emphasizes that this is agreed upon among all Muslims and is a foundational belief necessary for complete faith in God.

Key Terms
نبي (nabī)
رسول (rasūl)
وحي (waḥy)
جبريل (Jibrīl)
إلهام (ilhām)
يقظة (yaqaẓa)
رؤى (ruʾā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that prophetic wahy can include dreams or inspiration as initial proof of prophethood.
Correction: Buti clarifies that prophetic wahy is exclusively wakeful; dreams and inner feelings are not a basis for establishing prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to Buti, when do the dreams of a prophet become considered revelation (waḥy)?
A) They are always revelation from the beginning.
B) Only after the prophet's prophethood is established with definitive evidence.
C) Only if they contain a legal command.
D) They are never considered revelation.

Key Takeaway
The unified definition of prophets and messengers is agreed upon by all Muslims and rests on the reality of wakeful revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered a strong intuition or a vivid dream to be a form of divine guidance, even though this lesson teaches that prophetic wahy is exclusively wakeful and distinct from such experiences? How does that challenge your spiritual discernment, and what steps will you take to avoid conflating your own feelings with divine revelation?
?
The definition of a messenger includes a command to convey what was revealed. Are you actively fulfilling the duty of conveying the message of Islam to others, or have you become comfortable with merely being a recipient of knowledge without sharing it? What specific action will you take this week to convey even a single verse of the Quran to someone who does not know it?
?
Buti states that the definition of prophet and messenger is agreed upon by all Muslims and is a foundation for complete faith in God. If you have treated this definition as a mere academic detail rather than a living belief, how will you now integrate it into your conviction such that it strengthens your relationship with the prophets and with God?

Warning Against Modern Definitions and Critique of Muhammad Abduh
Buti warns against innovative definitions of nubuwwa and criticizes Abduh's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Buti sharply rejects modern definitions of prophethood, especially Muhammad Abduh's definition, and how he grounds his critique in the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna.

Core Idea
Any definition of prophethood that departs from the Qur'an, Sunnah, and Sunni consensus is either a conspiracy, weak faith, or ignorance.

Buti has just presented the classical Sunni definition of nubuwwa based on revelation through Jibril, wakefulness, etc. He now warns that any modern definition contradicting this is not merely a harmless reinterpretation but a dangerous deviation. He then targets a specific example: the definition of Shaykh Muhammad Abduh, narrated by Mustafa Sabri. This lesson examines Buti's warning and his critique of Abduh's 'strange invention'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the strong language: 'dasisah' (conspiracy), 'ḍaʿf balīgh' (severe weakness), 'jahl mutanāh' (utter ignorance).
2
Identify Buti's three categories
Buti lists three possible sources for any definition that contradicts the classical one: conspiracy, weak faith, or ignorance. Identify each category in the text.
This shows that Buti sees no room for legitimate scholarly disagreement on this core issue.
3
Examine Muhammad Abduh's definition
Read Abduh's definition as quoted by Buti. Note how he defines the prophet as one 'futira' (innately disposed) to truth, without need for discursive thought. Compare this to the classical definition based on waḥy.
Buti dismisses this as an 'ikhtirāʿ ʿajīb' (strange invention).
4
Reflect on the role of consensus
Buti grounds his rejection in the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamāʿa. Ask yourself: why is consensus so authoritative in matters of ʿaqīda? How does this relate to the warning against modern definitions?
This step connects the specific critique to the broader methodology of Islamic theology.

Then if after that you come across new 'modern' definitions of the prophet or revelation contrary to this which we have transmitted—all the books of Islamic creed based in their rulings on the certainties from the evidences of the Book and the Sunnah—then know that it is a conspiracy with much behind it, or it is a severe weakness in the faith of the writer or speaker, or it is an utter ignorance of the clearest Islamic truths. If you know this, you will not be taken in or deceived by the strange definition that Shaykh Muḥammad ʿAbduh invented for the prophet, as reported by the great scholar al-Ustādh Muṣṭafā Ṣabrī from his comments on the commentary of al-Jalāl al-Dawānī on al-ʿAqāʾid al-ʿAḍudiyya on page (3) when he said: 'I say: The prophet may be defined as a human being who is innately disposed (fuṭira) to truth in knowledge and action—such that he only knows truth and only acts truth in accordance with wisdom. That is through innate disposition (fiṭra), meaning he does not need discursive thought but divine teaching. And if he is also innately disposed to call his fellow humans to what he is naturally inclined to, then he is also a messenger.' And you have no need for me to comment on this strange invention of the meaning of prophethood after you have known its meaning as indicated by the Book and the Sunnah and as agreed upon by Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamāʿa; just as you have no need for me to disclose the secret that led him to use the word 'fiṭra' in the definition instead of (1) See the book 'Mawqif al-ʿAql wa al-ʿIlm min Rabb al-ʿĀlamīn' by al-Ustādh Muṣṭafā Ṣabrī: 4-40.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage serves as a warning against new, modern definitions of nubuwwa that contradict the classical Sunni consensus. Buti asserts that any definition differing from the one he presented (based on waḥy via Jibrīl, wakefulness, etc.) is either a conspiracy, a sign of weak faith, or ignorance. He then specifically attacks Muhammad Abduh's definition as narrated by Mustafa Sabri. Abduh defined the prophet as a human being 'innately disposed (futira) to truth in knowledge and action,' without needing discursive thought but receiving divine teaching directly. Buti considers this a 'strange invention' and says no further commentary is needed because the correct meaning is already established from the Qur'an, Sunnah, and consensus of Ahl al-Sunna. He also hints that the use of the word 'fiṭra' in the definition is suspect, but he declines to elaborate further, referring the reader to Sabri's work.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Modern redefinitions of prophethood are harmless intellectual exercises that can coexist with the classical definition.
Correction: Buti asserts that any definition contradicting the classical consensus is either a conspiracy, weak faith, or ignorance, and he dismisses Muhammad Abduh's definition as a 'strange invention' that should not be taken seriously.

Checkpoint

B) Because it contradicts the definition based on waḥy and the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna.

D) Because it requires discursive thought which is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The classical definition of nubuwwa is not open to reinterpretation; any deviation is a threat to the integrity of Islamic creed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refuting a Deviant Definition of Prophethood
Refutation of a modern definition of prophethood and reaffirmation of the necessity of revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Sunni tradition insists on revelation (wahy) as the essential criterion for prophethood, rejecting definitions that substitute innate disposition (fitra).

Core Idea
Prophethood is defined by divine revelation, not by innate disposition.

In this passage, the author warns against a modern redefinition of prophethood that replaces revelation with innate disposition. He presents Muhammad Abduh's definition, then refutes it by appealing to the Quran, Sunnah, and the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna. Understanding this critique helps clarify the essential role of wahy in distinguishing a prophet from a wise philosopher or saint.

Guided Path
1
Read Muhammad Abduh's definition
The passage begins by quoting Muhammad Abduh's definition of a prophet as one created upon truth by fitra, without need for thought, and if also created to call others, he is a messenger. Read this carefully.
Note that Abduh uses the word 'futira' (created with an innate disposition) rather than 'wahy'.
2
Identify the author's critique
The author dismisses this as a 'strange invention' and contrasts it with the true meaning derived from Quran, Sunnah, and consensus. Identify the key point: the substitution of fitra for wahy is the core error.
3
Understand the consensus on wahy
The author asserts that all Muslims, from the prophetic era to the present, have unanimously taken revelation (al-wahy) as an essential condition for defining a prophet. Consider why this is critical.
This consensus (ijma) is a foundational argument in Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: What does this redefinition imply about the nature of prophethood? Why would replacing wahy with fitra be problematic? The commentary will help you see that it risks conflating prophecy with innate human excellence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا علمت هذا فإنك لن تؤخذ أو تخدع بالتعريف العجيب الذي اخترعه الشيخ محمد عبده للنبي ، وذلك فيما نقله العلامة الأستاذ مصطفى صبري عن تعليقاته على شرح الجلال الدواني على العقائد العضدية في صفحة ( ۳ ) حينما قال : ( أقول : قد يعرف النبي بإنسان فطر على الحق علماً وعملاً . أي بحيث لا يعلم إلا حقاً ولا يعمل إلا حقاً على مقتضى الحكمة ، وذلك يكون بالفطرة ، أي لا يحتاج فيه إلى الفكر والنظر ولكن التعليم الإلهي . فإن فطر أيضاً على دعوة بني نوعه إلى ما جبل عليه فهو رسول أيضاً )) وأنت في غنى عن أن أعلق شيئاً على هذا الاختراع العجيب لمعنى النبوة ، بعد أن عرفت معناها كما دل عليه الكتاب ودلت السنة وكما أجمع عليه أهل السنة والجماعة ، كما أنك في غنى عن أن أكشف لك السر الذي دعاه إلى أن يستعمل كلمة الفطرة ، في التعريف بدلاً من كلمة ( الوحي ( التي أطبق المسلمون كلهم - منذ عصر النبوة إلى عصرنا هذا ـ على اتخاذها قيداً أساسياً في تعريف النبي .

That is, such that he knows only truth and does only truth in accordance with wisdom, and that is by innate disposition (fitra), i.e., he has no need for thought and reflection but rather divine teaching. Then if he is also created (futira) upon calling his fellow humans to what he is disposed to, then he is also a messenger.' And you have no need for me to comment on this strange invention for the meaning of prophethood, after you have known its meaning as indicated by the Book and indicated by the Sunna and as agreed upon by Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a, just as you have no need for me to reveal to you the secret that led him to use the word 'fitra' in the definition instead of the word 'wahy' (revelation) – which all Muslims, from the era of prophethood to our present era, have unanimously taken as an essential condition in defining a prophet.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a deviant definition of prophethood. The author begins by presenting Muhammad Abduh's definition, which defines a prophet as a human being created upon truth by fitra (innate disposition) without needing thought or reflection, only divine teaching. If such a person is also created upon calling others, he is a messenger. The author then rejects this definition, stating that the correct meaning of prophethood is derived from the Quran, Sunnah, and the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a. He points out that Abduh replaced the essential condition of 'wahy' (revelation) with 'fitra', while all Muslims have unanimously required revelation as the defining criterion. The author implies that this substitution is a dangerous innovation that undermines the nature of prophecy.

The Skeptics' Flight from Scientific Method
The author critiques the methodology of skeptics regarding revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how atheists and skeptics abandon scientific inquiry when dealing with revelation, and the importance of intellectual freedom.

Core Idea
Skeptics flee from reason when confronted with revelation.

The author begins by inviting us to examine the phenomenon of revelation in the Prophet's life. He points out that skeptics and atheists, when analyzing revelation, abandon the very scientific method they claim to uphold. Instead, they surrender to any illusion that allows them to deny prophethood. The author then emphasizes that we must approach this topic with pure intellectual freedom, free from bias or ulterior motives.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's critical tone toward skeptics.
2
Identify the skeptics' error
The author says skeptics flee from the scientific method and cling to any illusion to avoid acknowledging prophethood. Find the phrases that describe this.
Look for 'خلط وخبط واضطراب' and 'الاستسلام لأي وهم'.
3
Reflect on intellectual freedom
The author demands pure intellectual freedom from partisanship or ulterior motives. Why is this important for studying revelation?
Consider how bias can prevent objective understanding.

The Definition and Target of Revelation
The author defines revelation and explains why it is targeted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of revelation as the foundation of prophethood and why enemies of Islam seek to confuse it with other phenomena.

Core Idea
Revelation is the sole criterion between divine message and human opinion.

The author defines revelation as the foundation of prophethood and messengership. It is the primary source of all unseen knowledge, creed, and legislation. Revelation is the only distinction between a person who speaks from his own opinion and one who conveys from Allah without alteration. Because of this, enemies of Islam strive to confuse revelation with inspiration, self-talk, or epilepsy, as this undermines the source of Muslim certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the definition of revelation and its importance.
2
Understand the definition of revelation
Revelation is the foundation of prophethood and the source of all Islamic knowledge. It distinguishes divine communication from human opinion.
Look for 'الأساس الأول' and 'الفيصل الوحيد'.
3
Recognize why enemies attack revelation
Enemies know revelation is the source of Muslim certainty, so they try to confuse it with other phenomena.
They use phrases like 'التلبيس على حقيقته'.
4
Note the phenomena they confuse it with
The author lists inspiration (ilham), self-talk (hadith al-nafs), and even epilepsy (sara').
These are attempts to reduce revelation to ordinary human experiences.

ذلك فلنذكر كلمة موجزة حول ) ظاهرة الوحي ( في حياة الرسول ومع و ذلك كي تقف من وراء التأمل فيها على مدى ما يقع فيه الملاحدة ومحترفو التشكيك الديني ، من خلط وخبط واضطراب أثناء تحليلهم لهذه الظاهرة وكيف أنهم يندّون ويبتعدون عن الطريقة العلمية للبحث في أبسط مظاهرها وأشكالها ويرتضون لأنفسهم الاستسلام لأي وهم أو حدس وتخمين ، ما دام يبعدهم عن الاعتراف بنبوة محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام . ونحن لا نريد منك ، وأنت تتأمل في هذا البحث وحقيقته ، وكيفية روغان أعداء العقيدة الإسلامية عنه ، إلا أن تملك الحرية العقلية الصافية عن أي تبعية أو غرض أو هوى في نفسك . ☆ ظاهرة الوحي : الوحي هو الأساس الأول الذي يقوم على حقيقته معنى النبوة والرسالة ومن ثم فهو المنبع الأول لعامة الإخبارات الغيبية وشؤون العقيدة وأحكام التشريع ، ذلك أن حقيقة ( الوحي ) هي الفيصل الوحيد بين الإنسان الذي يفكر من عنده ويشرع بواسطة رأيه وعقله ، والإنسان الذي يبلغ عن ربه دون أن يغير أو ينقص أو يزيد . من أجل هذا يهتم أعداء الإسلام، بمعالجة موضوع الوحي في حياته صلى الله عليه وسلم ويبذلون جهداً فكرياً شاقاً ، في تكلف وتمحل ، من أجل التلبيس على حقيقته والخلط بينه وبين الإلهام وحديث النفس ، بل وحتى الصرع أيضاً ، وذلك لعلمهم بأن موضوع ( الوحي ) هو منبع يقين المسلمين وإيمانهم بما جاء به محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم من علومه
So let us mention a brief word about the phenomenon of revelation (zahirat al-wahy) in the life of the Messenger, and that so that by contemplating it you may appreciate the extent of the confusion, disorder, and disturbance that atheists and professional religious skeptics fall into while analyzing this phenomenon, and how they flee and distance themselves from the scientific method of research in its simplest manifestations and forms, and consent for themselves to surrender to any illusion, conjecture, or guesswork, as long as it distances them from acknowledging the prophethood of Muhammad, peace be upon him. And we do not want from you, while you contemplate this research and its reality, and how the enemies of the Islamic creed evade it, except that you possess pure intellectual freedom free from any partisanship, ulterior motive, or passion in your soul. ★ The phenomenon of revelation: Revelation is the first foundation upon which the reality of prophethood and messengership rests, and hence it is the primary source for all reports of the unseen, matters of creed, and rulings of legislation. This is because the reality of revelation is the sole criterion between a human who thinks on his own and legislates by means of his opinion and intellect, and a human who conveys from his Lord without changing, decreasing, or increasing. For this reason, the enemies of Islam pay attention to discussing the topic of revelation in the life of the Messenger, peace be upon him, and exert strenuous intellectual effort, with affectation and strained interpretation, in order to obscure its reality and confuse it with inspiration (ilham), self-talk (hadith al-nafs), and even epilepsy (sara'), because they know that the topic of revelation is the source of the Muslims' certainty and faith in what Muhammad, peace be upon him, brought from his sciences.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage serves as an introduction to the phenomenon of revelation. The author begins by stating his intention to discuss revelation in the life of the Prophet. He invites the reader to contemplate how atheists and skeptics mishandle the topic, abandoning the scientific method and resorting to illusions to avoid acknowledging Muhammad's prophethood. The author then emphasizes the importance of intellectual freedom, free from bias. Finally, he defines revelation as the foundation of prophethood and the sole criterion distinguishing divine communication from human opinion. He explains that enemies of Islam target revelation because it is the source of Muslim certainty, and they try to confuse it with lesser phenomena like inspiration or epilepsy.

Revelation: The Foundation of Prophethood
Buti defines revelation and refutes skeptical theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that revelation is the unique criterion distinguishing divine messaging from human opinion, and why skeptics' attempts to explain it away fail both logically and historically.

Core Idea
Revelation is not inspiration, self-talk, or epilepsy—it is divinely conveyed truth.

Buti opens by establishing revelation as the bedrock of prophethood and messengerhood. He then explains why this concept is a prime target for skeptics and proceeds to dismantle three common naturalistic explanations. Finally, he challenges the skeptics to produce evidence and reaffirms the historical basis of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of revelation
Read the opening where Buti defines revelation as the criterion between human legislation and divine communication. Notice how he emphasizes it is the sole criterion.
2
Understand why enemies attack revelation
Buti explains that enemies target revelation because it is the source of Muslim certainty. They try to confuse it with inspiration, self-talk, or epilepsy.
3
Identify the three false theories
The passage lists three skeptical theories. Find each one and note how Buti dismisses them without extensive refutation here, focusing instead on the lack of evidence.
4
Examine Buti's challenge and counter-argument
Buti challenges the skeptics to provide scientific evidence for their claims, pointing out their hypocrisy. Then he states the correct Islamic view: revelation is known through historical transmission (Quran, Sira, Sunnah).

The Phenomenon of Revelation: Revelation is the first foundation upon which the meaning of prophethood and messengerhood is established, and thus it is the primary source of all reports of the unseen, matters of creed, and rulings of legislation. That is because the reality of revelation is the sole criterion between the human who thinks from himself and legislates by his own opinion and intellect, and the human who conveys from his Lord without altering, reducing, or increasing. For this reason, the enemies of Islam concern themselves with addressing the subject of revelation in his life, and they expend strenuous intellectual effort, with affectation and fabrication, to obscure its reality and confuse it with inspiration, self-talk, and even epilepsy, because they know that the subject of revelation is the source of the certainty of Muslims and their faith in what Muhammad (peace be upon him) brought from Allah. If they could cause Muslims to doubt its reality, they could then cause them to disbelieve in all that branches out from it of creeds and rulings, and they could make them respond to the idea that everything Muhammad called to, of principles and legislative rulings, is nothing but his own thinking. To achieve this goal, the professionals of intellectual invasion attempt to interpret the phenomenon of revelation and distance it from what historians and the authentic Sunnah have narrated to us about it, and they try to strip it of its apparent reality. Each of them proceeds toward that by whatever fancies his imagination of strange, affected conceptions. Some imagine that Muhammad (peace be upon him) kept thinking until a doctrine gradually formed in his mind that he considered capable of eliminating paganism. Others prefer to spread the claim that he learned the Quran and the principles of Islam from Bahira the monk. Still others say that it is neither this nor that, but Muhammad was a nervous man or suffered from epilepsy. I believe that any rational person has the right to ask: what is the scientific proof that these imagers relied on to prove their claims about revelation and its reality? Especially since they accuse us, as you know, of basing our religious research solely on creed without science. So where is science, or even the semblance of science, in their research? As for us, we say: the source of the word 'revelation' in the life of Muhammad (peace be upon him) is the report transmitted to us through the Quran, the Sira, and the authentic Sunnah. Had the word not reached us from these sources, it would have no existence in our minds or in the minds of the enemies of Islam, and consequently no research would arise about it. (1) See: Hasir al-Alam al-Islami, vol. 1, pp. 28 and 187-29.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage defines the phenomenon of revelation (al-wahy) as the foundational concept of prophethood and messengerhood, and refutes skeptical theories that undermine its divine origin. The author, Ramadan Buti, begins by stating that revelation is the primary source of all reports of the unseen, creedal matters, and legislative rulings. He emphasizes that revelation is the exclusive criterion distinguishing a human who legislates from his own reason from a prophet who conveys God's message without alteration. Next, Buti explains why enemies of Islam attack the concept of revelation: they know it is the source of Muslim certainty. They attempt to confuse revelation with inspiration, self-talk, or epilepsy to cast doubt. If they succeed, they can cause Muslims to doubt all derivative beliefs and rulings, ultimately claiming that Muhammad's teachings are merely his own ideas. Buti then lists three false theories: (1) that Muhammad gradually developed his doctrine through continuous thinking to combat paganism; (2) that he learned the Quran and Islam from Bahira the monk; (3) that he suffered from nervousness or epilepsy. He challenges these theorists to provide scientific proof for their claims, noting their hypocrisy in accusing religious scholars of lacking science while they themselves offer no evidence. Finally, Buti asserts the correct Islamic position: the source of the word 'revelation' for Muhammad is the historical report transmitted via the Quran, Sira, and authentic Sunnah. Without these sources, the concept would be unknown. This establishes revelation as a historically attested fact, not a speculative theory.

C) The influence of the monk Bahira

Key Takeaway
Revelation is a historically attested divine communication, not a product of human thought, learning, or illness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What step can you take this week to deepen your knowledge of the Quran and Sunnah as the source of revelation?

The Goal of Intellectual Invasion and False Theories about Revelation
Author describes the goal of intellectual invasion and lists three false theories about revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ultimate goal of the intellectual invasion according to the author, and the three false theories it promotes to undermine the divine origin of revelation.

Core Idea
The intellectual invasion aims to strip revelation of its divinity to dismantle Islamic beliefs.

The author reveals the strategic aim behind the intellectual invasion: to cast doubt on the reality of revelation. Once that doubt is planted, all Islamic teachings become vulnerable. In this passage, he identifies three specific false theories that are propagated to achieve this goal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ultimate goal
Read the first part of the passage to find what the intellectual invasion aims to achieve regarding revelation.
The goal is to make Muslims doubt the divine origin of revelation.
2
Recognize the method
Notice how the author says the 'professionals of intellectual invasion' reinterpret revelation and distance it from historical accounts.
This method is called ta'wil (interpretation) but used illegitimately.
3
Examine the three false theories
The passage enumerates three alternative explanations of revelation. Identify each one and consider why they are considered 'strange and affected'.
The theories are: gradual self-discovery, learning from Bahira, and epilepsy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلئن أتيح تشكيك المسلمين بحقيقته أمكن تكفيرهم بكل ما قد يتفرع عقائد وأحكام، وأمكنهم أن يحملوهم على الاستجابة لفكرة أن كل ما دعا إليه محمد من المبادئ والأحكام التشريعية ليس إلا من تفكيره الذاتي. من أجل تحقيق هذه الغاية، أخذ محترفو الغزو الفكري يحاولون تأويل ظاهرة الوحي وإبعادها عما يرويه لنا بشأنها المؤرخون وترويه صحاح السنة الشريفة، كما يحاولون تجريدها عن حقيقتها الظاهرة، وراح كل منهم يسلك إلى ذلك ما يروق لخياله من فنون التصورات المتكلفة الغريبة. فمن متصور بأن محمداً (عليه الصلاة والسلام) لم يزل يفكر حتى تكونت في نفسه بطريقة الكشف التدريجي المستمر عقيدة كان يراها الكفيلة بالقضاء على الوثنية؛ ومن مفضل على ذلك إشاعة القول بأنه تعلم القرآن ومبادئ الإسلام من بحيرا الراهب؛ ومن قائل بأن الأمر ليس هذا ولا ذاك ولكن محمداً ما كان رجلاً عصبياً أو مصاباً بداء الصرع.
If it were possible to cast doubt among Muslims on its reality, it would be possible to declare them disbelievers in everything that branches off from it in beliefs and rulings, and it would be possible to make them respond to the idea that everything to which Muhammad called from principles and legislative rulings is nothing but his own thought. To achieve this goal, the professionals of intellectual invasion began attempting to interpret the phenomenon of revelation and distance it from what historians narrate to us about it and what the authentic books of the noble Sunna narrate, and they also attempt to strip it of its apparent reality, and each of them started following what pleased his imagination from strange and affected forms of perceptions. So among them is one who imagines that Muhammad (peace be upon him) kept thinking until a belief formed in his mind by way of continuous gradual discovery, which he thought capable of eliminating polytheism; and another who prefers to spread the claim that he learned the Qur'an and the principles of Islam from the monk Bahira; and another who says it is neither this nor that, but Muhammad was not a nervous man or suffering from epilepsy.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage identifies the ultimate goal of the intellectual invasion: to cast doubt on the divine origin of revelation (al-wahy) so that all Islamic beliefs and laws stemming from it become questionable. To achieve this, the author states that the 'professionals of intellectual invasion' attempt to reinterpret (ta'wil) the phenomenon of revelation, distancing it from the historical accounts found in the authentic sources of the Sunna and the Sira. They then present three alternative theories: first, that Muhammad's teachings gradually developed in his own mind through a process of natural discovery; second, that he learned the Qur'an and Islamic principles from the monk Bahira; and third, that he was simply a nervous or epileptic man. The author presents these as examples of 'strange and affected perceptions' (al-tasawwurat al-mutakallifah al-gharibah), implying they are baseless inventions.

Key Takeaway
The intellectual invasion deliberately promotes alternative explanations of revelation to undermine its divine origin.

Divine Wisdom in Revelation's Beginning: Four Questions
Author poses rhetorical questions about revelation's beginning and discerns divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the puzzling features of the beginning of revelation—the Prophet's fear, the suspicion of jinn, and the interruption—are actually manifestations of divine wisdom that establish certainty and safeguard truth.

Core Idea
The Prophet's initial fear and confusion in revelation were deliberately ordained wisdom, not weakness.

This passage from Shaykh Ramadān al-Būṭī invites us to consider four natural questions about the way revelation began. Instead of seeing the Prophet's fear, the suspicion of jinn, and the long pause as flaws, the author shows they are part of a profound divine plan that yields a certain, rationally grounded understanding, protecting believers from the fanciful interpretations of Islam's opponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the four questions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the four distinct questions the author asks about the beginning of revelation. Mark them in your mind.
These questions are rhetorical but pivotal for the argument.
2
Understand the contrast with Orientalist views
Notice how the author contrasts the authentic Islamic narrative with the 'strange imaginary things' put forward by Orientalists. This sets up the need for a rational, certain explanation.
The author implies that only the Islamic account can provide true certainty.
3
Reflect on the wisdom behind each question
For each question, think why the form of revelation might have been designed that way. Use the commentary to see how each detail serves a purpose in establishing an unshakeable faith.
The wisdom is not immediately obvious but becomes clear upon reflection.
4
Connect to the broader theme of certain knowledge
The author concludes that these details enable the thinker to reach 'the pure truth, based on the certain scientific method.' See how this protects against intellectual manipulation.
This ties the passage to the larger theme of yaqīnī knowledge in Islam.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن عندما نقارن بين تفسيرات هذه النصوص الحاسمة لظاهرة الوحي في حياته عليه الصلاة والسلام ) وفي مقدمتها حديث بدء الوحي ) وما يفسرهـا بـه أولئك المستشرقون والمخاصمون للإسلام من أمور خيالية عجيبة ، لا يرى العاقل يغاً للقول بها إلا التهرب من الإقرار بنبوته عليه الصلاة والسلام ـ ندرك في جلاء ووضوح الحكمة الإلهية الباهرة ، من بدء نزول الوحي عليه الصلاة والسلام بهذه الطريقة التي صح ورودها في حديث الإمام البخاري وغيره :
لماذا رأى رسول الله الله جبريل بعيني رأسه لأول مرة ، وقد كان بالإمكان أن يكون الوحي من وراء حجاب ؟
لماذا قذف الله في قلبه عليه الصلاة والسلام الرعب منه والحيرة في فهم حقيقته ، وقد كان ظاهر محبة الله لرسوله وحفظه له تقضي أن يلقي السكينة في قلبه ويربط على فؤاده فلا يخاف ولا يرتعد ؟ لماذا خشي على نفسه أن يكون هذا الذي ساوره طائفاً من الجن ولم يستيقن من أول الأمر أنه ملك أمين من عند الله ؟
لماذا انفصل الوحي عنه بعد ذلك مدة طويلة ، وجزع النبي ذلك جزعاً عظيماً حتى إنه كان يحاول ـ كما يروي الإمام البخاري ـ أن يتردى من شواهق الجبال ؟
هذه أسئلة طبيعية بالنسبة للشكل الذي ابتدأ به الوحي ، ولدى التفكير في أجوبتها نجدها تنطوي على حكمة : : ألا أن يجد المفكر الحرفيها الحقيقة الناصعة ، القائمة على المنهج العلمي اليقيني ، والواقية عن الوقوع في شرك محترفي الغزو الفكري والتأثر بأخيلتهم المتكلفة الباطلة
And when we compare these decisive interpretations of the phenomenon of revelation in his life (peace be upon him) (foremost among them the hadith of the beginning of revelation) with what those Orientalists and opponents of Islam interpret it with from strange imaginary things, which no rational person sees any motivation for saying except to escape from acknowledging his prophethood (peace be upon him) – we perceive clearly the overwhelming divine wisdom, from the beginning of revelation descending upon him (peace be upon him) in this way that has been authentically reported in the hadith of Imam al-Bukhari and others: Why did the Messenger of Allah see Jibril with his own eyes for the first time, while it could have been revelation from behind a veil? Why did Allah cast into his heart fear and confusion in understanding its reality, while apparent love of Allah for His Messenger and protection for him would require that He cast tranquility into his heart and strengthen his heart so that he would not fear nor tremble? Why did he fear that this which had come to him was a touch of the jinn and did not from the first moment become certain that it was a trusted angel from Allah? Why did revelation separate from him after that for a long period, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) became so distressed that he would try, as narrated by Imam al-Bukhari, to throw himself from mountain peaks? These are natural questions regarding the form in which revelation began, and upon thinking about their answers we find them containing wisdom: that the thinker may find in them the pure truth, based on the certain scientific method, and protecting from falling into the trap of professional intellectual invasion and influenced by their false fabricated imaginations.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage draws a contrast between the Islamic explanation of revelation (based on authentic hadith) and the imaginative explanations of Orientalists. The author argues that the specific form of the beginning of revelation—with its seemingly puzzling features—actually embodies divine wisdom. He poses four pointed questions: (1) Why was the first encounter with Jibril visual rather than veiled? (2) Why did the Prophet experience fear and confusion instead of tranquility? (3) Why did he initially suspect jinn rather than an angel? (4) Why was there a long interruption (fatrat al-waḥy) that caused extreme distress? These questions highlight the unusual nature of the event. The author asserts that the answers reveal wisdom: the true, certain understanding that protects against intellectual manipulation. The passage underscores that the Islamic narrative, when properly understood, provides a coherent and rational explanation that stands in contrast to the baseless fantasies of opponents.

بدء الوحي (badʾ al-waḥy)
المنهج العلمي اليقيني (al-manhaj al-ʿilmī al-yaqīnī)
الغزو الفكري (al-ghazw al-fikrī)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Prophet's fear, suspicion of jinn, and distress during the interruption indicate weakness, doubt, or confusion in his prophethood.
Correction: The author argues that these very features are part of divine wisdom, designed to establish a certain, scientifically rigorous faith that withstands intellectual attack. They are not signs of weakness but deliberate elements that ultimately strengthen the truth.

A) To test the Prophet's faith.

Key Takeaway
The specific form of revelation's beginning—with visual encounter, fear, initial uncertainty, and long interruption—was divinely designed to produce a certain, rationally grounded faith that shields against intellectual exploitation.

?
The passage warns that failing to see the wisdom in revelation's details can leave one vulnerable to intellectual invasion. What doubts about Islam have you entertained because you haven't explored the deeper reasoning? Will you commit to seeking the 'certain scientific method' in understanding your faith?
?

Wisdom in the Sudden and External Beginning of Revelation
Buti argues from the Prophet's initial fear that revelation was external and sudden.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the details of the first revelation (fear, confusion, pause) prove that it was an external divine event, not a psychological or gradual phenomenon.

Core Idea
The Prophet’s terror proves revelation came from outside, not from inside.

Buti asks why the Prophet reacted with fear and confusion at the first revelation. The answers reveal divine wisdom: they show that revelation was not a gradual inner development but a sudden external intervention. This lesson explores those questions and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four key questions
Buti poses four specific questions about the first revelation. Read carefully and list them in your mind. They set up the entire argument.
The questions are: Why see Gabriel with eyes? Why fear and confusion? Why fear of jinn? Why long pause?
2
Understand the wisdom behind each answer
For each question, Buti gives an answer that points to one conclusion: revelation is external and sudden. Note how each detail (seeing, fear, etc.) supports this.
3
Follow the syllogistic proof
Buti argues that gradual spiritual development never produces sudden terror. Since the Prophet experienced terror, his experience cannot be gradual inner development. Therefore it must be external.
This is a certain syllogism (qiyās yaqīnī) based on induction.
4
Reflect on involuntary reactions as proof
Fear, trembling, and paleness are involuntary. They cannot be faked. This further confirms the sincerity and external origin of the revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن عندما نقارن بين تفسيرات هذه النصوص الحاسمة لظاهرة الوحي في حياته عليه الصلاة والسلام ) وفي مقدمتها حديث بدء الوحي ) وما يفسرهـا بـه أولئك المستشرقون والمخاصمون للإسلام من أمور خيالية عجيبة ، لا يرى العاقل يغاً للقول بها إلا التهرب من الإقرار بنبوته عليه الصلاة والسلام ـ ندرك في جلاء ووضوح الحكمة الإلهية الباهرة ، من بدء نزول الوحي عليه الصلاة والسلام بهذه الطريقة التي صح ورودها في حديث الإمام البخاري وغيره :
لماذا رأى رسول الله الله جبريل بعيني رأسه لأول مرة ، وقد كان بالإمكان أن يكون الوحي من وراء حجاب ؟
لماذا قذف الله في قلبه عليه الصلاة والسلام الرعب منه والحيرة في فهم حقيقته ، وقد كان ظاهر محبة الله لرسوله وحفظه له تقضي أن يلقي السكينة في قلبه ويربط على فؤاده فلا يخاف ولا يرتعد ؟ لماذا خشي على نفسه أن يكون هذا الذي ساوره طائفاً من الجن ولم يستيقن من أول الأمر أنه ملك أمين من عند الله ؟
لماذا انفصل الوحي عنه بعد ذلك مدة طويلة ، وجزع النبي ذلك جزعاً عظيماً حتى إنه كان يحاول ـ كما يروي الإمام البخاري ـ أن يتردى من شواهق الجبال ؟
هذه أسئلة طبيعية بالنسبة للشكل الذي ابتدأ به الوحي ، ولدى التفكير في أجوبتها نجدها تنطوي على حكمة : : ألا أن يجد المفكر الحرفيها الحقيقة الناصعة ، القائمة على المنهج العلمي اليقيني ، والواقية عن الوقوع في شرك محترفي الغزو الفكري والتأثر بأخيلتهم المتكلفة الباطلة
لقد فوجئ محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام وهو في غار حراء بجبريل أمامه يراه بعينه ، وهو يقول له اقرأ ، حتى يتبين أن ظاهرة الوحي ليست أمراً ذاتياً داخلياً مرده إلى حديث النفس المجرد ، وإنما هي استقبال وتلق لحقيقة خارجية لا علاقة لها بالنفس وداخل الذات . وضم الملك إياه ثم إرساله ثلاث مرات ، قائلاً في كل مرة : اقرأ ، يعد تأكيداً لهذا التلقي الخارجي ومبالغة في نفي ما قد يتصور ، من أن الأمر لا يعدو كونه خيالاً داخلياً فقط
ولقد داخله الخوف والرعب مما سمع ورأى ، حتى إنه قطع خلوته في الغار وأسرع عائداً إلى البيت يرجف فؤاده، لكي يتضح لكل مفكر عاقل أن رسول الله لم يكن متشوقاً للرسالة التي سيدعى إلى حملها وبثها في العالم ، وأن ظاهرة الوحي هذه لم تأت منسجمة أو متممة لشيء مما قد كان يتصوره أو يخطر في باله وإنما طرأت طروءاً مثيراً على حياته ، وفوجئ بها ( بالرسالة ) دون أي توقع سابق
ولا شك أن هذا ليس شأن من يتدرج في التأمل والتفكير إلى أن تتكون في نفسه - بطريقة الكشف التدرجي المستمر - عقيدة يؤمن بالدعوة إليها ثم إن شيئاً من حالات الإلهام أو حديث النفس أو الإشراق الروحي أو التأملات العلوية لا يستدعي الخوف والرعب واصفرار اللون . يدل على ذلك القياس اليقيني القائم على استقراء الحالات وجميع الظروف المشابهة وليس ثمة أي انسجام بين التدرج في التفكير والتأمل من ناحية ، ومفاجأة الخوف والرعب من ناحية أخرى ، وإلا للزم من ذلك أن يعيش جميع المفكرين والمتأملين نهباً لدفعات من الرعب والخوف المفاجئة المتلاحقة
وأنت خبير أن الخوف والرعب ورجفان الجسم وتغير اللون ـ كل ذلك من الانفعالات القسرية التي لا سبيل إلى اصطناعها والتمثيل بها
And when we compare between the interpretations of these decisive texts regarding the phenomenon of revelation in his life, peace be upon him — foremost among them the hadith of the beginning of revelation — and what those Orientalists and opponents of Islam interpret it with from strange imaginary matters, the intelligent person sees no face for saying them except escaping from affirming his prophethood, peace be upon him — we perceive clearly and obviously the brilliant divine wisdom, from the beginning of the descent of revelation upon him, peace be upon him, in this manner that has been authentically reported in the hadith of Imam al-Bukhari and others:
Why did the Messenger of God see Gabriel with his own eyes for the first time, when it could have been possible that revelation be from behind a veil?
Why did God cast into his heart, peace be upon him, fear of him and confusion in understanding his reality, when the apparent love of God for His Messenger and His protection of him would require that He cast tranquility into his heart and strengthen his heart so that he would not fear nor tremble? Why did he fear for himself that this which had come upon him might be a visitation from the jinn, and he was not certain from the first that it was a trustworthy angel from God?
Why did revelation separate from him after that for a long period, and the Prophet, peace be upon him, was extremely distressed by that, to the extent that he would try — as al-Bukhari narrates — to throw himself from mountain peaks?
These are natural questions regarding the form by which revelation began, and upon thinking about their answers we find that they contain wisdom: that the thoughtful researcher may find the pure truth, based on the certain scientific method, and protecting from falling into the trap of professional intellectual invasion and being influenced by their strained false imaginations.
Muhammad, peace be upon him, was suddenly faced while in the Cave of Hira with Gabriel before him, seeing him with his own eyes, saying to him 'Read', so that it becomes clear that the phenomenon of revelation is not an internal subjective matter referable to mere self-talk, but rather it is receiving and encountering an external reality having no relation to the self and the interior of the soul. And the angel's embracing him and then releasing him three times, saying each time 'Read', is a confirmation of this external reception and an exaggeration in negating what might be imagined, that the matter is nothing but an internal imagination only.
And fear and terror entered him from what he heard and saw, until he cut short his seclusion in the cave and hurried back to the house with his heart trembling, so that it becomes clear to every rational thinker that the Messenger of God was not eager for the message that he would be called to carry and disseminate in the world, and that this phenomenon of revelation did not come in harmony with or as a completion of anything that he might have imagined or that might have crossed his mind, but rather it occurred as a startling occurrence in his life, and he was suddenly faced with it (the message) without any prior expectation.
And there is no doubt that this is not the case of one who gradually progresses in reflection and thinking until a belief that he believes in and calls to is formed in his soul — by way of continuous gradual uncovering — and then something of the states of inspiration or self-talk or spiritual illumination or lofty reflections does not necessitate fear and terror and paleness of color. This is indicated by certain syllogism based on induction of cases and all similar circumstances. And there is no harmony between gradualness in thinking and reflection on the one hand, and the suddenness of fear and terror on the other hand, otherwise it would necessitate that all thinkers and contemplatives live as prey to successive sudden outbursts of terror and fear.
And you are well aware that fear, terror, trembling of the body and change of color — all of that are involuntary emotions that cannot be feigned or imitated.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is an extended argument from wisdom (ḥikma) about the beginning of revelation (badʾ al-waḥy). The author, Ramadan al-Buti, presents a series of questions about the details of the event as recorded in authentic hadith (especially the hadith of the beginning of revelation narrated by al-Bukhari) and then explains the wisdom behind each detail. The central thesis is that the external and terrifying nature of the first revelation proves that it was not a product of the Prophet's own mind or a gradual inner development, but a real, external, and sudden intervention from God.

The argument proceeds as follows:
1. First, Buti sets up the context: Orientalists and opponents interpret the Prophet's experience in fantastic ways to avoid admitting his prophethood. By contrast, the authentic reports reveal divine wisdom.
2. He then poses four key questions:
   - Why did the Prophet see Gabriel with his own eyes, rather than receiving revelation from behind a veil?
   - Why was the Prophet filled with fear and confusion, when God's love for him would seem to require tranquility?
   - Why did he fear it might be from the jinn, and was not immediately certain it was an angel?
   - Why did revelation stop for a long period, causing the Prophet such distress that he considered suicide?
3. The answers to these questions reveal wisdom: they show that revelation is not an internal psychological phenomenon but an external event. The physical seeing and the embrace by the angel emphasize this external reception. The fear and uncertainty demonstrate that the Prophet was not predisposed to or eager for the message; he was genuinely surprised. The pause in revelation (fatrat al-waḥy) further underscores that this was not a self-generated progression but a divine intervention that came and went on God's terms.
4. Buti then makes a key argument: gradual contemplation and reflection do not produce sudden fear and terror. If revelation were the result of the Prophet's own spiritual development, it would not have caused such violent reactions. On the contrary, the involuntary physical responses (fear, trembling, pale complexion) are impossible to feign, proving the sincerity and external origin of the experience.
5. He concludes with a syllogistic argument: all cases of gradual spiritual insight are free from such terror; the Prophet's experience involved terror; therefore it was not a case of gradual insight. The emotional reactions are involuntary and cannot be artificially produced, further supporting the authenticity of the external revelation.

The passage is a defense of the orthodox Islamic understanding of revelation against those who would reduce it to a psychological or spiritual phenomenon.

Key Terms
بدء الوحي
ظاهرة الوحي
الوحي الخارجي
حديث النفس
الخوف والرعب
الحكمة الإلهية
القياس اليقيني
استقراء الحالات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Prophet's initial fear and confusion indicate he was uncertain or that revelation was a gradual self-hypnosis.
Correction: Buti argues that the fear and confusion actually prove the opposite: they show that revelation was an external, sudden event, not a gradual inner development, and that the Prophet had no prior expectation or eagerness.

Checkpoint
According to Buti, why did the Prophet experience such intense fear and terror during the first revelation?
A) To show that the Prophet was not eager for the message and that revelation was an external event, not self-generated.
B) Because Gabriel was terrifying in appearance and the Prophet was naturally afraid of angels.
C) Because the Prophet was sinful and feared punishment.
D) Because the Prophet was physically weak and could not handle the experience.

Key Takeaway
The external and sudden nature of revelation, proven by the Prophet's involuntary fear and the break, establishes that it is not a product of his mind but a genuine divine event.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Qur'an or follow the Sunnah, do you truly treat them as external commands from God—even when they contradict your own desires—or do you subtly filter them through what feels comfortable to you, as if they were just your own thoughts? What would it take for you to obey an uncomfortable command today, knowing it comes from outside you?
?
The Prophet was terrified and uncertain at the beginning of revelation, not eager or self-assured. Have you ever used your own spiritual 'experiences' or feelings as a substitute for the revealed law, assuming that your inner state is a guide? How can you guard against confusing your own emotions with divine guidance?
?
The Prophet's involuntary reactions proved he was not feigning. Think of a time you have pretended to be more pious or certain than you really are—in prayer, in giving advice, or in public. Will you commit to being honest with yourself and with God about your actual state, even if it is fear and weakness, rather than performing spirituality?

The External Nature of the First Revelation
Buti argues that revelation is an external phenomenon using the encounter with Jibril.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Buti uses the Prophet's encounter with Jibril to prove that revelation is an external, objective event rather than a subjective mental experience.

Core Idea
Revelation is an external encounter, not internal imagination.

Buti begins by recounting the first revelation in the cave of Hira. The Prophet saw Jibril with his own eyes and heard the command 'Read.' This vivid description serves a deeper theological purpose: to establish that revelation (waḥy) is not a product of the mind but a real external event. We will examine how the angel's physical actions—embracing and releasing the Prophet three times—function as evidence for this claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's description of the event come through clearly.
Notice the emphasis on 'seeing him with his own eyes'.
2
Identify the two opposing views
The passage contrasts an 'internal subjective matter' with 'external reality'. Underline the phrases that represent each view. Ask yourself: what does Buti want to prove?
3
Analyze the angel's embrace as evidence
Pay close attention to the angel's action of embracing and releasing the Prophet three times. Why does Buti call this a 'confirmation' and an 'exaggeration'? How does it refute the idea that revelation was just imagination?
This step connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لقد فوجئ محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام وهو في غار حراء بجبريل أمامه يراه بعينه ، وهو يقول له اقرأ ، حتى يتبين أن ظاهرة الوحي ليست أمراً ذاتياً داخلياً مرده إلى حديث النفس المجرد ، وإنما هي استقبال وتلق لحقيقة خارجية لا علاقة لها بالنفس وداخل الذات . وضم الملك إياه ثم إرساله ثلاث مرات ، قائلاً في كل مرة : اقرأ ، يعد تأكيداً لهذا التلقي الخارجي ومبالغة في نفي ما قد يتصور ، من أن الأمر لا يعدو كونه خيالاً داخلياً فقط
Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, was surprised while in the cave of Hira with Jibril before him, seeing him with his own eyes, saying to him: 'Read,' so that it becomes clear that the phenomenon of revelation is not an internal subjective matter traced back to mere self-talk, but rather it is receiving and encountering an external reality having no connection to the soul and the inner self. The angel’s embracing him and then releasing him three times, each time saying 'Read,' is a confirmation of this external reception and an exaggeration in negating what might be imagined, that the matter is nothing but an internal imagination only.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the external reality of revelation. Buti begins by describing the Prophet's first encounter with Jibril in the cave of Hira: he saw the angel and heard the command 'Iqra' (Read). The purpose of this description is to establish that revelation (waḥy) is not an internal psychological phenomenon (dhātī dākhilī) originating from self-talk (ḥadīth al-nafs), but rather a reception (istiqbāl) and encounter (talaqqī) of an external reality (ḥaqīqa khārijiyya) independent of the soul. The key evidence is the angel's physical action: embracing the Prophet (ḍamma) and then releasing him three times, each time repeating the command. This repetition and physical contact serve as a confirmation (ta’kīd) of the external reception and an exaggeration (mubālagha) in denying any suggestion that the event was merely an internal fantasy (khayāl dākhilī). The argument is that such a vivid, repeated, and physically interactive experience cannot be reduced to a product of the mind.

Key Terms
وحي
جبريل
غار حراء
حديث النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim revelation was a product of Muhammad's own mind.
Correction: Buti refutes this by emphasizing the external encounter and physical actions of Jibril.

Checkpoint

B) That revelation is an external phenomenon, not internal imagination.
C) That the Prophet needed to learn to read.
D) That Jibril was testing the Prophet's endurance.

Key Takeaway
The first revelation was an external event proven by the angel's physical presence and repeated command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Qur'an came to Muhammad from outside himself, through a real angel, how does that truth demand a different response from you when you recite its verses? Will you today treat each verse as a direct external command to you?
?
The Prophet was physically embraced and commanded three times. When has Allah's guidance 'embraced' you forcefully, and did you resist it? Will you now surrender to the repeated commands in your life?
?
You might have doubted whether the Qur'an is truly from Allah. After seeing this proof of its external origin, what will you do to remove that doubt permanently? Commit to one specific action today.
